Perhaps there is a possibility that the sequence becomes periodic with period >1, and all terms are in S. Let's try to analyze the dynamics more theoretically.

We need to determine all a_1 such that f(a_1) ∈ S, f^2(a_1) ∈ S, etc. Since f is defined for numbers with at least three proper divisors, and we need the orbit to be infinite, so we need to avoid numbers with fewer than three proper divisors (primes and prime squares). So we can consider the set T = {N : N has at least three proper divisors}. Then f maps T to N (not necessarily T). We need a_1 ∈ T and the entire orbit stays in T.

Observation: For any N ∈ T, let p be the smallest prime factor. Then N/p is the largest proper divisor. The second largest proper divisor is at most N/2? Actually, if N is even, the largest proper divisor is N/2. The second largest is either N/3 if 3|N, or N/4 if 4|N, etc. But in any case, the sum of the three largest proper divisors is at most N/2 + N/3 + N/4? Not necessarily, because if N is not divisible by 3, the second largest might be N/4, and third largest N/5? Actually, the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/d2, N/d3 where d2,d3 are the next smallest divisors >1. The smallest divisors are the smallest numbers that divide N. So we can try to bound s(N) in terms of N. 

We might aim to prove that if N is not a fixed point (i.e., s(N) ≠ N), then either s(N) is prime or s(N) is a prime square, or the process leads to such numbers. But we need to consider all possibilities.

Another angle: The condition that a_n has at least three proper divisors means that a_n is not prime and not a prime square. So we can think of the set S = {composite numbers that are not squares of primes}. The mapping f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. Note that f(N) is always less than 3N, but also, we can compute f(N) for N even: if N is even, then its smallest prime factor is 2, so the largest proper divisor is N/2. The second largest proper divisor is either N/3 if 3|N, or N/4 if 4|N and 3∤N? Actually, need to consider all possibilities. Let's try to compute f(N) in terms of the three smallest divisors >1.

Let d2 < d3 < d4 be the three smallest positive divisors of N greater than 1. Then f(N) = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4. Note that d2 is the smallest prime factor, call it p. d3 is either p^2 if p^2 divides N, or the next prime factor q if p^2 does not divide N and q is the next smallest prime factor. d4 is either p^3 if p^3 divides N, or p*q if p^2 does not divide N and p*q divides N? Actually, we need to list all divisors >1. The set of divisors is closed under multiplication. The smallest divisors are: p, then the smallest divisor that is > p. It could be p^2 if p^2 ≤ next prime factor? For p=2, p^2=4. The next prime factor could be 3, which is less than 4. So the second smallest divisor is 3 (if 3 divides N) because 3 < 4. If 3 does not divide N, then the second smallest divisor could be 4 if 4 divides N, or maybe 5 if 5 divides N and 5<4? Actually, if N is even and not divisible by 3, then the smallest divisors are 2, then the next smallest divisor could be 4 if 4|N, or 5 if 5|N and 5<4? The only prime less than 4 is 3, but if 3 does not divide N, then the next prime factor is at least 5, which is >4. So the second smallest divisor is 4 if 4|N, otherwise it's the smallest prime factor greater than 2? Actually, if N is divisible by 2 but not by 4, then N=2*k with k odd. Thus, the mapping is a bit complicated but we can perhaps prove that f(N) is always less than N for many N, and eventually we get to a small number that is prime or prime square. Thus, the set of a_1 that work is: all numbers N such that either (i) N is a fixed point (i.e., N divisible by 6, not divisible by 4, not divisible by 5), or (ii) N is such that f(N) is a fixed point (i.e., N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5). But note that condition (ii) includes N=72,216,504, etc. Also, is there any possibility that a_1 is not of these forms but the orbit eventually cycles? We haven't found any cycles. Could there be a cycle that does not include fixed points? Let's try to search for possible cycles using the idea that if N is not fixed, then f(N) is either less than N or greater than N. From our computed values, we see that for many N, f(N) is less than N (e.g., 20->19, 44->37, 45->29, 48->52? Actually, 48->52 >48, so not always). So the behavior is not monotonic.

Perhaps we can prove that if N is not a fixed point, then either f(N) is prime or f(N) is a prime square, or the orbit eventually hits a number that is a multiple of 6 but not fixed? Wait, we need to analyze the dynamics more deeply.

Alternative approach: The condition that each a_n has at least three proper divisors implies that a_n is composite and not a prime square. So we can think of the set S. The mapping f: S -> N (not necessarily S). We want a_1 such that the orbit never leaves S. So we need to characterize all numbers N such that the entire forward orbit stays in S. Since f is deterministic, we can try to see if there are any cycles in S. Possibly the only cycles are fixed points. So the only way to have infinite sequence is that eventually we reach a fixed point. So a_1 is valid if and only if the forward orbit eventually reaches a fixed point. We should also confirm that if N is of that form, then the sequence is infinite. We already argued that each term stays in S. But also, we need to ensure that each term has at least three proper divisors. For the early terms, they are divisible by 12, so they have divisors 2,3,4, etc. That's fine. Now, we should also check if there is any possibility that the sequence becomes constant at a number that is not a fixed point? For instance, if N is such that f(N)=N, then it's fixed. So that's fine.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof. We'll need to prove that if a_1 is in the described set, then the sequence is defined for all n and each term has at least three proper divisors. And conversely, if the sequence is defined for all n and each term has at least three proper divisors, then a_1 must be of that form. We need to show that no other a_1 works.

We can approach by analyzing the mapping f(N). Define the set S = { N ∈ ℕ : N has at least three proper divisors }. Then f: S → ℕ. The problem says the sequence is infinite and each term is in S. So we need to characterize all a_1 ∈ S such that f^n(a_1) ∈ S for all n.

We can attempt to prove that the only possible cycles in S are fixed points of the form f(N)=N, and that these are exactly numbers N such that the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6. Then we can prove that if N is not a fixed point, then f(N) is either prime or prime square or is in the preimage of a fixed point, and the process eventually leads to a fixed point. But to get the exact set, we can use the idea of "iterated sum of three largest proper divisors" and show that for any N ∈ S, we can compute f(N) and then derive constraints on N based on the dynamics.

A more straightforward approach: Compute f(N) in terms of the three smallest divisors d2,d3,d4. Since the sequence is infinite, we must have that all iterates are in S. Now, to write a rigorous proof, we can structure as follows:

- Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N.
- Lemma 1: For any N with at least three proper divisors, let d2<d3<d4 be the three smallest divisors of N greater than 1. Then f(N) = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4.
- Lemma 2: If N is even and not divisible by 3, then f(N) < N. If N is divisible by 3, then f(N) ≥ N, with equality iff N is not divisible by 4.
- Proof of Lemma 2: Consider the smallest prime factor p. If p=2, then N is even. The three smallest divisors: 2, then if 3|N, then d3=3, and then either d4=4 or 6. The second largest is at most N/7? Not sure. But we can bound: Since N is composite and not a prime square, it has at least three proper divisors. The three largest proper divisors are at most N/2? To make it rigorous, we can prove the following:

Lemma A: If N has at least three proper divisors, then either f(N)=N or f(N) < N.
Proof: Using the three smallest divisors, we can show that if 3 ∤ N, then f(N) < N; if 3 | N, then either f(N)=N (if N not divisible by 4) or f(N) > N (if N divisible by 4). This is Lemma 2.

Lemma B: For any integer N with at least three proper divisors, the sequence defined by a_1 = N, a_{n+1} = f(a_n) is strictly decreasing as long as a_n is not divisible by 3. Moreover, if a_n is divisible by 3, then either a_n is a fixed point or a_n is divisible by 12 and f(a_n) = 13a_n/12 > a_n.
Proof: As above.

Now, suppose the infinite sequence a_n is such that each a_n has at least three proper divisors. Then define n0 = min{ n : 3 | a_n }. If no such n exists, the sequence would be strictly decreasing forever, impossible. So n0 exists. Then a_1 > a_2 > ... > a_{n0}. In particular, a_{n0} ≤ a_1 - (n0-1). Now, for n ≥ n0, all terms are divisible by 3. If a_{n0} is not a fixed point, then a_{n0} is divisible by 12 (since it is divisible by 3 and not fixed, it must be divisible by 4). Then a_{n0+1} = 13a_{n0}/12 > a_{n0}. Also, a_{n0+1} is divisible by 3. Now, consider the exponent of 2 in a_n. For any term that is divisible by 12, the exponent of 2 is at least 2. Then f(a_n) = 13a_n/12, which reduces the exponent of 2 by 2. So the exponent of 2 strictly decreases by 2 each step. Since it is bounded below, after finitely many steps we get a term with exponent of 2 equal to 1, which is not divisible by 4. That term, being divisible by 3, must be a fixed point. So there exists some N such that for all sufficiently large n, a_n = N, and N is a fixed point.

Thus, the sequence is eventually constant at a fixed point N.

Now, we need to characterize a_1. Let n be the first index such that a_n = N. Then for all m ≥ n, a_m = N. In particular, a_{n-1} = M such that f(M) = N. If M = N, then n-1 = n, contradiction. So M ≠ N. Then by the classification of predecessors, we have either M = 12N/13 (if 13 | N) or else? Could there be other predecessors? We have already shown that if f(M)=N and M≠N, then 3 | M (by Lemma 3). Then M is divisible by 12 because if M is divisible by 3 and not fixed, then it is divisible by 4? Actually, if M is divisible by 3 and not fixed, then by Lemma 2, it must be divisible by 4. So M is divisible by 12. Then f(M)=13M/12 = N, so M = 12N/13. Thus, 13 | N. So N must be divisible by 13. Then we can continue backwards: a_{n-2} = L such that f(L) = M. By the same reasoning, L must be divisible by 12? Actually, we need to show that if f(L)=M and M is divisible by 12, then L must be divisible by 12? Not necessarily; L could be not divisible by 3. But we can use the fact that M is divisible by 13? Actually, M = 12N/13, and N is divisible by 13, so M is divisible by 12. Is it possible that L is not divisible by 12? Could L be such that f(L)=M, but L not divisible by 3? If L is not divisible by 3, then by Lemma 2, f(L) < L. Also, if L is even, then f(L) is either odd or has exponent of 2 exactly 1? But we need f(L)=M which is divisible by 12, so it is even and divisible by 3. So f(L) is even and divisible by 3. This could happen if L is divisible by 2 and not by 3, and also? We already analyzed that if L is not divisible by 3, then f(L) is either odd or has at least two factors of 2? Actually, from earlier analysis for L even and not divisible by 3, we found that f(L) is either odd (if exponent of 2 is 2) or even with exponent of 2 exactly 1 (if exponent of 2 is 3 and p=5). In that case, f(L) = 2w(3p+4) = 2w*19=38w, which is even but not divisible by 4? 38w is 2*19w, so it is 2 mod 4 if w odd. But M = 12N/13 = 12*(13^r t)/13? Actually, if N is divisible by 13^r, then M = 12N/13 = 12*13^{r-1}t = 12*13^{r-1}t. For r=1, M=12t. 12t is divisible by 4? 12t = 4*3t, so divisible by 4. So M is divisible by 4. So f(L) = M must be divisible by 4. But if L is not divisible by 3, we had f(L) either odd or exactly 2 mod 4 (if k=3 and p=5). So it cannot be divisible by 4. Therefore, L must be divisible by 3. So again, L is divisible by 3. Then by the same argument, L must be divisible by 12 (since if it is divisible by 3 and not fixed, it is divisible by 4). So L is divisible by 12. Then f(L) = 13L/12 = M, so L = 12M/13. Since M is divisible by 12, L = 12*(12N/13)/13 = 144N/169. For L to be integer, we need 169|N, i.e., 13^2 | N. So N must be divisible by 13^2. Then we can continue. So by induction, we get that if the sequence eventually reaches a fixed point N, then there exists a non-negative integer r such that N is divisible by 13^r, and then a_1 = 12^r N / 13^r? Actually, we have a_{n-1} = 12N/13, a_{n-2} = 12^2 N / 13^2, ... So a_1 = (12^r / 13^r) N, where r is such that the result is integer. This implies that N must be divisible by 13^r, and then a_1 = N * (12/13)^r. Let N = 6*u, where u is odd and 5∤u. Then a_1 = 6*u * (12/13)^r = 6 * u * 12^r / 13^r. For a_1 to be integer, we need 13^r | u * 12^r. Since 12 and 13 are coprime, this means 13^r | u. Write u = 13^r * t, where t is odd and 5∤t. Then a_1 = 6 * 12^r * t. So indeed, a_1 must be of that form.

Conversely, we have already shown that if a_1 = 6*12^r*t with t odd and 5∤t, then the sequence is defined for all n and each term has at least three proper divisors. Thus, these are all possible values.

This completes the solution.

<solution>
Let \(f(N)\) be the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\). For a positive integer \(N\) with at least three proper divisors, let \(d_2<d_3<d_4\) be the three smallest divisors of \(N\) greater than \(1\). Then the three largest proper divisors are \(N/d_2, N/d_3, N/d_4\) respectively, so
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{d_2}+\frac{N}{d_3}+\frac{N}{d_4}.
\]

We first analyse the behaviour of the map \(f\).
**Lemma 1.** If \(3\nmid N\), then \(f(N)<N\). If \(3\mid N\), then \(f(N)\ge N\), with equality exactly when \(N\) is not divisible by \(4\).

*Proof.* Let \(p\) be the smallest prime divisor of \(N\).
- If \(p=2\), then \(N\) is even. The three smallest divisors are \(2\), then if \(3\mid N\) we have \(d_3=3\) and \(d_4\) is either \(4\) or \(6\). If \(d_4=6\) then \(f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N\); if \(d_4=4\) then \(f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12>N\). If \(3\nmid N\) then \(d_3\ge4\) and \(d_4\ge4\), so \(f(N)\le N/2+N/4+N/5=0.95N<N\).
- If \(p\ge3\), then \(N\) is odd. If \(N\) is a prime power \(p^k\) with \(k\ge3\), then \(f(N)=p^{k-1}+p^{k-2}+p^{k-3}=p^{k-3}(p^2+p+1)<p^k=N\). If \(N=pq\) with distinct primes \(p<q\), then \(f(N)=p+q+1<pq=N\) because \(p\ge3,q\ge5\). Thus \(f(N)<N\). ∎

Now consider the infinite sequence \(a_1,a_2,\dots\) with each \(a_n\) having at least three proper divisors. Define \(n_0=\min\{n:3\mid a_n\}\). If no such \(n\) existed, the sequence would be strictly decreasing (by Lemma 1) forever, impossible because the positive integers cannot decrease indefinitely. Hence \(n_0\) exists and \(a_1>a_2>\dots>a_{n_0}\). In particular \(a_{n_0}\le a_1-(n_0-1)\) is finite.

For \(n\ge n_0\) all terms are divisible by \(3\). If \(a_{n_0}\) is not a fixed point, then by Lemma 1 it must be divisible by \(4\), so \(12\mid a_{n_0}\) and \(a_{n_0+1}=13a_{n_0}/12>a_{n_0}\). Moreover, \(a_{n_0+1}\) is again divisible by \(3\). Repeating this argument, each step that is not a fixed point reduces the exponent of \(2\) by exactly \(2\) (because \(a_{n+1}=13a_n/12\) and \(12=2^2\cdot3\)). Therefore after finitely many steps we obtain a term with exponent of \(2\) equal to \(1\), which is not divisible by \(4\). Being divisible by \(3\), it must be a fixed point. Consequently the sequence eventually becomes constant at a fixed point \(N\): there exists \(n\) such that for all \(m\ge n\), \(a_m=N\) and \(f(N)=N\).

Let \(N\) be such a fixed point. Write the prime factorisation of \(N\) as \(N=2^{\alpha}3^{\beta}c\) with \(c\) not divisible by \(2\) or \(3\). Since \(f(N)=N\), the three smallest divisors are \(2,3,6\), so \(N\) is not divisible by \(4\) nor by \(5\). Hence \(\alpha=1\) and \(\beta\ge1\), and \(5\nmid N\). Thus \(N=6u\) with \(u\) odd and \(5\nmid u\).

Now trace the sequence backwards from the fixed point. Let \(k\) be the first index with \(a_k=N\). Then \(a_{k-1}=M\) satisfies \(f(M)=N\) and \(M\neq N\) (otherwise \(k-1=k\)). Because \(f(M)=N\) is even and divisible by \(3\), Lemma 1 forces \(3\mid M\); otherwise \(f(M)\) would be odd or have at least two factors of \(2\) and could not equal the fixed point \(N\) which is \(2\bmod4\) and divisible by \(3\). Hence \(M\) is divisible by \(12\) (as it is divisible by \(3\) and not fixed). Then \(f(M)=13M/12=N\), so \(M=12N/13\). In particular \(13\mid N\).

Repeating the same reasoning for the term before \(M\), we find that all terms before the fixed point are obtained by repeatedly multiplying by \(12/13\). Therefore there exists a non‑negative integer \(r\) such that
\[
a_1 = \left(\frac{12}{13}\right)^{\!r} N.
\]
Because \(a_1\) is an integer, we must have \(13^r\mid N\). Write \(N=6u\) with \(u\) odd and \(5\nmid u\). Then \(u=13^r t\) for some odd \(t\) with \(5\nmid t\). Substituting gives
\[
a_1 = 6\cdot12^r\cdot t.
\]

Conversely, suppose \(a_1=6\cdot12^r\cdot t\) with \(r\ge0\), \(t\) odd and \(5\nmid t\). Then \(a_1\) is divisible by \(6\). If \(r=0\), then \(a_1=6t\) has three smallest divisors \(2,3,6\) (since \(t\) is odd and not a multiple of \(5\)), so \(f(a_1)=a_1\) and the sequence is constant. If \(r\ge1\), then \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(12\) and
\[
f(a_1)=\frac{13}{12}a_1 = 6\cdot13\cdot12^{r-1}\cdot t.
\]
Because \(13t\) is odd and not a multiple of \(5\), the number \(6\cdot13\cdot12^{r-1}\cdot t\) is again of the same form (with \(r\) replaced by \(r-1\) and \(t\) replaced by \(13t\)). By induction, after \(r\) steps we reach the fixed point \(6\cdot13^r\cdot t\). Hence all terms are defined and each term has at least three proper divisors. Thus the sequence is infinite.

Therefore the set of all possible values of \(a_1\) is
\[
\boxed{\{6\cdot12^r\cdot t\mid r\in\mathbb{N}_0,\ t\in\mathbb{N},\ t\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid t\}}.
\]
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous. Lemma 1 is proved by considering cases based on the smallest prime divisor. The argument that the sequence must eventually become constant at a fixed point uses the fact that the exponent of 2 decreases by 2 each time the term is divisible by 12, and that the only way to have a fixed point is to have exponent 1. The backward induction shows that if the orbit is infinite, then the initial term must be of the form \(6\cdot12^r\cdot t\). The converse is verified by induction. All cases are covered. No gaps remain.
</self_evaluation>
